5.7 on IMDB- wow, i truly can no longer trust IMDB- great movie
As a cinematographer with over 10 years of experience in camerawork, editing, and color grading, I have to applaud the look and feel of this movie. I love the experimental camera angles and the fluidity of the shots. From a technical standpoint, I truly enjoyed this movie.  However, when I evaluate it purely as a viewer, I find the movie incredibly bland, overly philosophical, and unengaging. I'm not very familiar with Adam Sandler's work, but I felt he didn't convey his character's emotions effectively (in terms of facial expressions, body language, and manner of speech).  The entire performance felt very artificial, as if I were watching one of my interns play a role. This repeatedly took me out of the experience. It felt like I only saw 10% of a movie, while the other 90% was missing. If that makes sense? NGL I hated this movie.
When you've been spoiled by Kubrick & Tarkovsky it's difficult to take anything that may appear less, more seriously. Sandler's Howard Ratner in Uncut Gems was probably his pinnacle. After that it's watered down versions of the same or lateral versions. And here it would seem Solaris gets a new spin too, but unfortunately it's only gravitational forces & zero G. Saying that it all and sounds great or generally good. Where Tarkovsky went poetical & Kubrick technical Spaceman gets lost in space in the middle - and in some communist subplot too, which seems to be neither here nor there. The talking spider eventually becomes annoying with the 'skinny human' routine repeated too often - and Sandler isn't exactly skinny either. The earth bound pregnancy turns a nice philosophy on what's important - yet both points are as well: science & the human race, so it feels aimless. And finally there's something homogenised about films from Netflix - it's as though the same people with the same Netflix remit churn out the same 'feeling' about its product. These endeavours maybe costly but there's no risk - no innovation, it's all one of the same. There's absolutely no room for the auteur. Instead there's a kind of cinematic margarine made to consume on the small screen. Perhaps a better title would have been Lost in Space, Again.' The last excellent space film was probably Duncan Jones's Moon - which somehow utilised the Tarkovsky/Kubrick conundrum with an understated grace & humility.

Do not watch this film; it is one of the worst movies I have ever seen. The film is incredibly boring with a poorly developed plot. It's just another Adam Sandler film, and even in a space-themed movie with aliens, he manages to revolve the story around his family (his wife). Despite Adam Sandler delivering some good performances in the past, this ranks among his worst. Nothing about it felt genuine; it was extremely unbelievable, even for a sci-fi genre. The main reason for his isolation in space is not explained at all.  The subplot involving the spider needed much more explanation; it suddenly appears without any backstory, except that it comes from an extinct species. And what's the deal with the purple cloud? Why do they have to fly there? Why go alone? The love story was irritating, with constant references to "I need to get to Lenka, I need to talk to her, what about Lenka." Moreover, the concept seems like a poorly copied version of Interstellar and another alien film, but much worse.  I hope this comment saves some people from watching the movie. I strongly advise against it.

Possible spoilers:  Really not sure what this movie is trying to say - other than the usual sci-fi trope of putting the most emotionally distraught astronaut into an extended space mission, hoping he can resolve all his deep personal problems - rather than focusing on something of interest for the audience.  I get it.  We need personal drama in space to give the story a human touch - but this exact same movie could be done if Adam Sandler was on an automated oil rig, or manning an arctic station, of exploring a huge underground cave - all on planet Earth.  The outer space setting adds nothing to the story.  On a positive note - the movie looks good.  Nice sets, well-done zero-g scenes, and some beautiful FX shots - but the story lacks punch for sci-fi fans who were hoping for some fresh ideas.  All that said, Sandler is pretty good in this - and I can't think of anyone doing better with the script they gave him.  Of course, your space mileage may vary.

Overall I liked the movie but it's not a great movie. The whole "God's Loneliest Astronaut" has been done before, better, in other films but the dialog between Adam Sandler and Paul Dano (who plays the space spider) are very compelling to watch. The movie is centered around Sandler's relationship with his wife played by Carey Mulligan (who is unfortunately wasted here, she doesn't get much to do) and yet the real narrative thrust is between Sandler and Dano. I just wish the ending had a stronger emotional landing than it did, it sort of drowns in its own philosophical word soup and doesn't come to any true catharsis or emotional upswell, it just sort of keeps the same tepid moodyness the rest of the film set. I still think it's worth a watch.

Repetitive and clichÃ©d scripts abound, showcasing Hollywood's apparent struggle to innovate in depicting the imaginative aspects of aliens, from their body structure to face, voice, and overall presence. .Hollywood struggles to produce fresh and distinctive narratives in space movies, evident in the strikingly similar storylines prevalent in the genre. The recurring theme involves a protagonist or main character grappling with the isolation of their loved ones in space. Additionally, the portrayal of aliens adheres to a predictable and unimaginative concept, often depicted as spider-like creatures. This pattern suggests that scriptwriters face challenges in presenting aliens with diverse characteristics, including their shape, voice, face, and overall structure.

Roman Sadovskyi: "If we try to be objective, the film is not outstanding and does not offer anything new. It's an attempt to make Netflix's own Interstellar. Visually, the film looks very good, and I also liked Paul Dano's spider. I also can't help but mention the powerful soundtrack by Max Richter and the final song he recorded with the band Sparks. However, the film is quite straightforward in its narrative - a story and flashbacks, which is a bit too little to try to reach the level of Christopher Nolan's Interstellar. From the very beginning, the main idea, which was already present in Andrei Tarkovsky's Solaris, becomes clear: space is big and lonely, and the Earth is our home. Spaceman is an incredibly beautiful and fascinating story, full of emotional depth and a re-experiencing of well-known truths. The film has a slogan: "I want to go home", which cannot but resonate with me as a person who found himself far from home because of the war in Ukraine. Because I want to go home too".

Eh, Idk about this one.  First off; if Adam is playing an astronaut that "obviously" volunteered" for this mission the trailer shows, WHY on earth would you do this mission at a crucial time that your fixing your marriage?  Also Im not an astronaut myself but I have read and done enough research to know that when these guys go into space, they go through intense training prior to heading off to a space station and with that they are trained not to fear, worry, or focus on family business when it comes to work related business.  I mean, sure; Nasa usually will provide methods of live feed or recorded messages the astronauts are allowed to send back to their families from time to time as a "hello from space" message but in most cases both sides of the families understand that the father/husband, etc are out doing their job.  All Im seeing in this trailer is a guy who had a rough marriage and decides "Well, off to space I go to get away from it all" and then all of a sudden as he's out in space for this xxx days mission, he suddenly is "Lonely" and decides then he wants to fix his marriage and be with his wife.  To me that is pretty lame and dumb of a story to write up on; but to each their own I guess.  Think I'll pass on this one.

This is my headcanon:  I am absolutely convinced that Adam Sandler is the one man that really figured out life.  He realized it takes minimum effort to amass huge piles of money doing horrible "comedies", so he does exactly that to get in with the cool kids in Hollywood. Once there, he can choose absolutely any role he wants, when he really wants it, and prove all the naysayers that he is an incredible actor.  Bear in mind, he's worked with some really great directors and writers out there. Paul Thomas Anderson, Noah Baumbach, the Zafdie Brothers, and now Johan Renck, they all had Sandler in drama form. And in all of the times Sandler has done dramatic roles, he's really knocked it out of the park.  But never often enough that we get bored of it. He knows his comedies don't transcend, and they are not memorable. That's not their point. But I bet he knows his drama roles keep blowing people away, so he does them, to prove the artist he really is, and to reap the absolute delight of knowing everyone never expects the depths he can bring to bear when truly acting.  I mean, his dramatic roles are so infrequent, that whenever we see him doing one, everyone loses their minds. And they all go to position themselves as incredible performances.  I've got nothing but respect for that man.
